	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Near East</h1>
	
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"></div>
		
		
		
		<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
		<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Geographical term that roughly encompasses Western Asia</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">This article is about the geographical term. For the geopolitical region that it applies to, see <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a>. For another geographical term that covers much of the region, see <a href="/wiki/Western_Asia" title="Western Asia">Western Asia</a>. For the ancient history of the region, see <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Near_East" title="Ancient Near East">Ancient Near East</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Near_East_topographic_map-blank.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Near_East_topographic_map-blank.svg/300px-Near_East_topographic_map-blank.svg.png" decoding="async" width="300" height="196" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Near_East_topographic_map-blank.svg/450px-Near_East_topographic_map-blank.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Near_East_topographic_map-blank.svg/600px-Near_East_topographic_map-blank.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="2425" data-file-height="1581" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Near_East_topographic_map-blank.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Near East topographic map</div></div></div>
<p>The <b>Near East</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Eurocentrism" title="Eurocentrism">Eurocentric</a><sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> geographical term which roughly encompasses a transcontinental <a href="/wiki/Region" title="Region">region</a> comprising <a href="/wiki/Western_Asia" title="Western Asia">Western Asia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a> (both <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolia</a> and <a href="/wiki/East_Thrace" title="East Thrace">East Thrace</a>), and <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a> (which is mostly located in <a href="/wiki/North_Africa" title="North Africa">North Africa</a>). Despite having varying definitions within different academic circles, the term was originally applied to the maximum extent of the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a>. The term has fallen into disuse in <a href="/wiki/American_English" title="American English">American English</a> and has been replaced by the terms <i><a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a></i>, which includes Egypt, and Western Asia, which includes <a href="/wiki/Transcaucasia" title="Transcaucasia">Transcaucasia</a>.
</p><p>According to the <a href="/wiki/National_Geographic_Society" title="National Geographic Society">National Geographic Society</a>, the terms <i>Near East</i> and <i>Middle East</i> denote the same territories and are "generally accepted as comprising the countries of the <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Peninsula" title="Arabian Peninsula">Arabian Peninsula</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a>, Egypt, <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>, <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jordan" title="Jordan">Jordan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lebanon" title="Lebanon">Lebanon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Palestinian_territories" title="Palestinian territories">Palestinian territories</a>, <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a>, and Turkey".<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> As of 1997, the <a href="/wiki/Food_and_Agriculture_Organization_of_the_United_Nations" class="mw-redirect" title="Food and Agriculture Organization of the United Nations">Food and Agriculture Organization</a> (FAO) of the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> defined the region similarly, but also included <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Eastern_Question">Eastern Question</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Near_East&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Eastern Question">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>At the beginning of the 19th century the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a> included all of the <a href="/wiki/Balkans" title="Balkans">Balkans</a>, south to the southern edge of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Hungarian_Plain" title="Great Hungarian Plain">Great Hungarian Plain</a>. But by 1914, the Empire had lost all of it except <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Thrace" class="mw-redirect" title="Eastern Thrace">Eastern Thrace</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Rise_of_nationalism_in_the_Ottoman_Empire" title="Rise of nationalism in the Ottoman Empire">rise of nationalist Balkan states</a>, which saw the independence of the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Greece" title="Kingdom of Greece">Kingdom of Greece</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Serbia" title="Kingdom of Serbia">Kingdom of Serbia</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Danubian_Principalities" title="Danubian Principalities">Danubian Principalities</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Bulgaria" title="Kingdom of Bulgaria">Kingdom of Bulgaria</a>. Up until 1912, the Ottomans retained a band of territory including <a href="/wiki/Albania" title="Albania">Albania</a>, <a href="/wiki/Macedonia_(region)" title="Macedonia (region)">Macedonia</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Adrianople_Vilayet" title="Adrianople Vilayet">Adrianople Vilayet</a>, which were lost in the two <a href="/wiki/Balkan_Wars" title="Balkan Wars">Balkan Wars</a> of 1912–13.
</p><p>The Ottoman Empire, believed to be about to collapse, was portrayed in the press as the "<a href="/wiki/Sick_man_of_Europe" title="Sick man of Europe">sick man of Europe</a>". The Balkan states, with the partial exception of <a href="/wiki/Bosnia_and_Herzegovina" title="Bosnia and Herzegovina">Bosnia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Albania" title="Albania">Albania</a>, were primarily Christian, as was the majority of <a href="/wiki/Lebanon" title="Lebanon">Lebanon</a>. Starting in 1894, the Ottomans struck at the <a href="/wiki/Armenians" title="Armenians">Armenians</a> on the explicit grounds that they were a non-Muslim people and as such were a potential threat to the Muslim empire within which they resided. The <a href="/wiki/Hamidian_Massacres" class="mw-redirect" title="Hamidian Massacres">Hamidian Massacres</a> aroused the indignation of the entire Christian world. In the United States the now aging <a href="/wiki/Julia_Ward_Howe" title="Julia Ward Howe">Julia Ward Howe</a>, author of the <a href="/wiki/Battle_Hymn_of_the_Republic" title="Battle Hymn of the Republic">Battle Hymn of the Republic</a>, leaped into the war of words and joined the <a href="/wiki/Red_Cross" class="mw-redirect" title="Red Cross">Red Cross</a>. Relations of minorities within the Ottoman Empire and the disposition of former Ottoman lands became known as the "<a href="/wiki/Eastern_Question" title="Eastern Question">Eastern Question</a>", as the Ottomans were on the east of Europe.
</p><p>It now became relevant to define the east of the eastern question. In about the middle of the 19th century <i>Near East</i> came into use to describe that part of the east closest to Europe. The term <i><a href="/wiki/Far_East" title="Far East">Far East</a></i> appeared contemporaneously meaning <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>, <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>, <a href="/wiki/Korea" title="Korea">Korea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a>. <i>Near East</i> applied to what had been mainly known as the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a>, which was in the jurisdiction of the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Porte" class="mw-redirect" title="Ottoman Porte">Ottoman Porte</a>, or government. Those who used the term had little choice about its meaning. They could not set foot on most of the shores of the southern and central Mediterranean from the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Sidra" title="Gulf of Sidra">Gulf of Sidra</a> to <a href="/wiki/Albania" title="Albania">Albania</a> without permits from the Ottoman Empire.
</p><p>Some regions beyond the Ottoman Porte were included. One was North Africa west of Egypt. It was occupied by piratical kingdoms of the <a href="/wiki/Barbary_Coast" title="Barbary Coast">Barbary Coast</a>, <i>de facto</i>-independent since the 18th century, formerly part of the empire at its apogee. <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a> was included because it could not easily be reached except through the Ottoman Empire or neighboring Russia. In the 1890s the term tended to focus on the conflicts in the Balkan states and Armenia. The demise of "the sick man of Europe" left considerable confusion as to what was to be meant by "Near East". It is now generally used only in historical contexts, to describe the countries of <a href="/wiki/Western_Asia" title="Western Asia">Western Asia</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean</a> to (or including) <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> There is, in short, no universally-understood fixed inventory of nations, languages or historical assets defined to be in it.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Background">Background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Near_East&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:B)_Syria_-_Belka,_Woman_from_Damascus,_Arab_from_Baghdad.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/B%29_Syria_-_Belka%2C_Woman_from_Damascus%2C_Arab_from_Baghdad.jpg/200px-B%29_Syria_-_Belka%2C_Woman_from_Damascus%2C_Arab_from_Baghdad.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="252" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/B%29_Syria_-_Belka%2C_Woman_from_Damascus%2C_Arab_from_Baghdad.jpg/300px-B%29_Syria_-_Belka%2C_Woman_from_Damascus%2C_Arab_from_Baghdad.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/B%29_Syria_-_Belka%2C_Woman_from_Damascus%2C_Arab_from_Baghdad.jpg/400px-B%29_Syria_-_Belka%2C_Woman_from_Damascus%2C_Arab_from_Baghdad.jpg 2x" data-file-width="414" data-file-height="522" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:B)_Syria_-_Belka,_Woman_from_Damascus,_Arab_from_Baghdad.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Inhabitants of the Near East, late 19th century</div></div></div>
<p>The geographical terms <i>Near East</i> and <i>Far East</i> referring to areas of the globe in or contiguous to the former <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a> and the neighboring colonies of the Dutch, Portuguese, Spanish and Germans, fit together as a pair based on the opposites of far and near, suggesting that they were innovated together. They appear together in the journals of the mid-19th century. Both terms were used before then with local British and American meanings: the near or far east of a field, village or shire.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ideas_of_the_east_up_to_the_Crimean_War">Ideas of the east up to the Crimean War</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Near_East&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Ideas of the east up to the Crimean War">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There was a linguistic predisposition to use such terms. The Romans had used them in near Gaul / far Gaul, near Spain / far Spain and others. Before them the Greeks had the habit, which appears in <a href="/wiki/Linear_B" title="Linear B">Linear B</a>, the oldest known script of Europe, referring to the near province and the far province of the kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Pylos" title="Pylos">Pylos</a>. Usually these terms were given with reference to a geographic feature, such as a mountain range or a river.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">Ptolemy</a>'s <i>Geography</i> divided Asia on a similar basis. In the north is "Scythia this side of the Himalayas" and "Scythia beyond the Himalayas".<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> To the south is "India on this side of the Ganges" and "India beyond the Ganges".<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Asia began on the coast of <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolia</a> ("land of the rising Sun"). Beyond the <a href="/wiki/Ganges" title="Ganges">Ganges</a> and <a href="/wiki/Himalayas" title="Himalayas">Himalayas</a> (including the <a href="/wiki/Tien_Shan" class="mw-redirect" title="Tien Shan">Tien Shan</a>) were <a href="/wiki/Serica" title="Serica">Serica</a> and <a href="/wiki/Serae" title="Serae">Serae</a> (sections of <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>) and some other identifiable far eastern locations known to the voyagers and geographers but not to the general European public.
</p><p>By the time of John Seller's <i>Atlas Maritima</i> of 1670, "India Beyond the Ganges" had become "the <a href="/wiki/East_Indies" title="East Indies">East Indies</a>" including China, Korea, southeast Asia and the islands of the Pacific in a map that was every bit as distorted as Ptolemy's, despite the lapse of approximately 1,500 years.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> That "east" in turn was only an English translation of Latin <i>Oriens</i> and <i>Orientalis</i>, "the land of the rising Sun", used since Roman times for "east". The world map of <a href="/wiki/Jodocus_Hondius" title="Jodocus Hondius">Jodocus Hondius</a> of 1590 labels all of Asia from the Caspian to the Pacific as <i>India Orientalis</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> shortly to appear in translation as the East Indies.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Reception_ceremony_of_the_Conte_de_Saint_Priest_at_the_Ottoman_Porte_Antoine_de_Favray_1767.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Reception_ceremony_of_the_Conte_de_Saint_Priest_at_the_Ottoman_Porte_Antoine_de_Favray_1767.jpg/300px-Reception_ceremony_of_the_Conte_de_Saint_Priest_at_the_Ottoman_Porte_Antoine_de_Favray_1767.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="223" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Reception_ceremony_of_the_Conte_de_Saint_Priest_at_the_Ottoman_Porte_Antoine_de_Favray_1767.jpg/450px-Reception_ceremony_of_the_Conte_de_Saint_Priest_at_the_Ottoman_Porte_Antoine_de_Favray_1767.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Reception_ceremony_of_the_Conte_de_Saint_Priest_at_the_Ottoman_Porte_Antoine_de_Favray_1767.jpg/600px-Reception_ceremony_of_the_Conte_de_Saint_Priest_at_the_Ottoman_Porte_Antoine_de_Favray_1767.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2371" data-file-height="1761" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Reception_ceremony_of_the_Conte_de_Saint_Priest_at_the_Ottoman_Porte_Antoine_de_Favray_1767.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Porte" class="mw-redirect" title="Ottoman Porte">Ottoman Porte</a>, 1767, gateway to trade with the Levant. Painting by <a href="/wiki/Antoine_de_Favray" title="Antoine de Favray">Antoine de Favray</a>.</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_I_of_England" title="Elizabeth I of England">Elizabeth I of England</a>, primarily interested in trade with the east, collaborated with English merchants to form the first trading companies to the far-flung regions, using their own jargon. Their goals were to obtain trading concessions by treaty. The queen chartered the Company of Merchants of the Levant, shortened to  <a href="/wiki/Levant_Company" title="Levant Company">Levant Company</a>, and soon known also as The Turkey Company, in 1581. In 1582, the ship <i>The Great Susan</i> transported the first ambassador, William Harebone, to the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Porte" class="mw-redirect" title="Ottoman Porte">Ottoman Porte</a> (government of the Ottoman Empire) at <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Compared to Anatolia, <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a> also means "land of the rising sun", but where Anatolia always only meant the projection of land currently occupied by the Republic of Turkey, Levant meant anywhere in the domain ruled by the Ottoman Porte. The <a href="/wiki/East_India_Company" title="East India Company">East India Company</a> (short for a much longer formal name) was chartered in 1600 for trade to the East Indies.
</p><p>It has pleased western historians to write of a <a href="/wiki/Decline_of_the_Ottoman_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Decline of the Ottoman Empire">decline of the Ottoman Empire</a> as though a stable and uncontested polity of that name once existed. The borders did expand and contract but they were always dynamic and always in "question" right from the beginning. The Ottoman Empire was created from the lands of the former <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">eastern Roman Empire</a> on the occasion of the latter's violent demise. The last Roman emperor died fighting hand-to-hand in the streets of his capital, Constantinople, overwhelmed by the Ottoman military, in May, 1453. The victors inherited his remaining territory in the Balkans.
</p><p>The populations of those lands did not accept Turkish rule. The Turks to them were foreigners with a completely different set of customs, way of life, and language. Intervals when there was no unrest were rare. The <a href="/wiki/Lands_of_the_Hungarian_Crown" title="Lands of the Hungarian Crown">Hungarian lands</a> under Turkish rule had become part of the <a href="/wiki/Habsburg_Monarchy" title="Habsburg Monarchy">Habsburg Monarchy</a> by 1688. in the <a href="/wiki/Great_Turkish_War" title="Great Turkish War">Great Turkish War</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Serbian_Revolution" title="Serbian Revolution">Serbian Revolution</a>, 1804–1833. created modern Serbia. The <a href="/wiki/Greek_War_of_Independence" title="Greek War of Independence">Greek War of Independence</a>, 1821–1832, created modern Greece, which recovered most of the lands of ancient Greece, but could not gain Constantinople. The Ottoman Porte was continuously under attack from some quarter in its empire, primarily the Balkans. Also, on a number of occasions in the early 19th century, American and British warships had to attack the <a href="/wiki/Barbary_pirates" title="Barbary pirates">Barbary pirates</a> to stop their piracy and recover thousands of enslaved Europeans and Americans.
</p><p>In 1853 the Russian Empire on behalf of the Slavic Balkan states began to question the very existence of the Ottoman Empire. The result was the <a href="/wiki/Crimean_War" title="Crimean War">Crimean War</a>, 1853–1856, in which the British Empire and the French Empire supported the Ottoman Empire in its struggle against the incursions of the Russian Empire. Eventually, the Ottoman Empire lost control of the Balkan region.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Original_diplomatic_concept_of_near_east">Original diplomatic concept of near east</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Near_East&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Original diplomatic concept of near east">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Men_of_47th_Regiment_in_winter_dress_crimea_c_1855.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Men_of_47th_Regiment_in_winter_dress_crimea_c_1855.jpg/250px-Men_of_47th_Regiment_in_winter_dress_crimea_c_1855.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="254" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Men_of_47th_Regiment_in_winter_dress_crimea_c_1855.jpg/375px-Men_of_47th_Regiment_in_winter_dress_crimea_c_1855.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Men_of_47th_Regiment_in_winter_dress_crimea_c_1855.jpg/500px-Men_of_47th_Regiment_in_winter_dress_crimea_c_1855.jpg 2x" data-file-width="637" data-file-height="648" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Men_of_47th_Regiment_in_winter_dress_crimea_c_1855.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>British troops, Crimea, 1855</div></div></div>
<p>Until about 1855 the words near east and far east did not refer to any particular region. The far East, a phrase containing a noun, East, qualified by an adjective, far, could be at any location in the "far east" of the speaker's home territory. The Ottoman Empire, for example, was the far East as much as the East Indies. The <a href="/wiki/Crimean_War" title="Crimean War">Crimean War</a> brought a change in vocabulary with the introduction of terms more familiar to the late 19th century. The Russian Empire had entered a more aggressive phase, becoming militarily active against the Ottoman Empire and also against China, with territorial aggrandizement explicitly in mind. Rethinking its policy the British government decided that the two polities under attack were necessary for the balance of power. It therefore undertook to oppose the Russians in both places, one result being the Crimean War. During that war the administration of the British Empire began promulgating a new vocabulary, giving specific regional meaning to "the Near East", the Ottoman Empire, and "the Far East", the East Indies. The two terms were now compound nouns often shown hyphenated.
</p><p>In 1855 a reprint of a letter earlier sent to <i><a href="/wiki/The_Times" title="The Times">The Times</a></i> appeared in <i>Littel's Living Age</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Its author, an "official Chinese interpreter of 10 years' active service" and a member of the <a href="/wiki/Oriental_Club" title="Oriental Club">Oriental Club</a>, Thomas Taylor Meadows, was replying to the suggestion by another interpreter that the British Empire was wasting its resources on a false threat from Russia against China. Toward the end of the letter he said:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886047036">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>To support the "sick man" in the Near East is an arduous and costly affair; let England, France and America too, beware how they create a "sick giant" in the Far East, for they may rest assured that, if Turkey is [a] European necessity, China is a world necessity.
</p></blockquote>
<p>Much of the colonial administration belonged to this club, which had been formed by the <a href="/wiki/Duke_of_Wellington" class="mw-redirect" title="Duke of Wellington">Duke of Wellington</a>. Meadows' terminology must represent usage by that administration. If not the first use of the terms, the letter to the <i>Times</i> was certainly one of the earliest presentations of this vocabulary to the general public. They became immediately popular, supplanting "Levant" and "East Indies", which gradually receded to minor usages and then began to change meaning.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Original_archaeological_concept_of_nearer_east">Original archaeological concept of nearer east</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Near_East&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Original archaeological concept of nearer east">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:HenryRawlinson.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/HenryRawlinson.JPG/220px-HenryRawlinson.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="276" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d7/HenryRawlinson.JPG 1.5x" data-file-width="295" data-file-height="370" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:HenryRawlinson.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Rawlinson</div></div></div>
<p><i>Near East</i> remained popular in diplomatic, trade and journalistic circles, but a variation soon developed among the scholars and the men of the cloth and their associates: <i>the Nearer East</i>, reverting to the classical and then more scholarly distinction of <i>nearer</i> and <i>farther</i>. They undoubtedly saw a need to separate the biblical lands from the terrain of the Ottoman Empire. The Christians saw the country as the land of the Old and New Testaments, where Christianity had developed. The scholars in the field of studies that eventually became <a href="/wiki/Biblical_archaeology" title="Biblical archaeology">biblical archaeology</a> attempted to define it on the basis of archaeology.
</p><p>For example, <i>The London Review</i> of 1861 (Telford and Barber, unsigned) in reviewing several works by <a href="/wiki/Sir_Henry_Rawlinson,_1st_Baronet" class="mw-redirect" title="Sir Henry Rawlinson, 1st Baronet">Rawlinson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Austen_Henry_Layard" title="Austen Henry Layard">Layard</a> and others, defined themselves as making: "... an imperfect conspectus of the arrow-headed writings of the nearer east; writings which cover nearly the whole period of the postdiluvian Old Testament history&#160;..."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETelfordBarber186114_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETelfordBarber186114-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> By arrow-headed writings they meant <a href="/wiki/Cuneiform" title="Cuneiform">cuneiform</a> texts. In defense of the Bible as history they said: "The primeval nations, that piled their glorious homes on the Euphrates, the Tigris, and the Nile, are among us again with their archives in their hands;&#160;..."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETelfordBarber18616_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETelfordBarber18616-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> They further defined the nations as "... the countries lying between the Caspian, the Persian Gulf, and the Mediterranean&#160;..."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETelfordBarber18617_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETelfordBarber18617-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> The regions in their inventory were Assyria, Chaldea, Mesopotamia, Persia, Armenia, Egypt, Arabia, Syria, Ancient Israel, Ethiopia, Caucasus, Libya, Anatolia and Abyssinia. Explicitly excluded is India. No mention is made of the Balkans.
</p><p>The British archaeologist <a href="/wiki/David_George_Hogarth" title="David George Hogarth">D.&#160;G. Hogarth</a> published <i>The Nearer East</i> in 1902, in which he stated his view of "The Near East":<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHogarth19021_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHogarth19021-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The Nearer East is a term of current fashion for a region which our grandfathers were content to call simply The East. Its area is generally understood to coincide with those classic lands, historically the most interesting on the surface of the globe, which lie about the eastern basin of the Mediterranean Sea; but few probably could say offhand where should be the limits and why.
</p></blockquote>
<p>Hogarth then proceeds to say where and why in some detail, but no more mention is made of the classics. His analysis is <a href="/wiki/Geopolitics" title="Geopolitics">geopolitical</a>. His map delineates the Nearer East with regular lines as though surveyed. They include Iran, the Balkans, but not the Danube lands, Egypt, but not the rest of North Africa.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> Except for the Balkans, the region matches the later Middle East. It differs from the Ottoman Empire of the times in including Greece and Iran. Hogarth gives no evidence of being familiar with the contemporaneous initial concept of the Middle East.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:No_original_research" title="Wikipedia:No original research"><span title="The material near this tag possibly contains original research. (May 2016)">original research?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Balkan_confusion">Balkan confusion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Near_East&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Balkan confusion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the last years of the 19th century the term <i>Near East</i> acquired considerable disrepute in eyes of the English-speaking public as did the Ottoman Empire itself. The cause of the onus was the religiously motivated <a href="/wiki/Hamidian_Massacres" class="mw-redirect" title="Hamidian Massacres">Hamidian Massacres</a> of Christian <a href="/wiki/Armenia" title="Armenia">Armenians</a>, but it seemed to spill over into the protracted conflicts of the Balkans. For a time, "Near East" meant primarily the Balkans.  Robert Hichens' book The Near East (1913) is subtitled Dalmatia, Greece and Constantinople.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sir_Henry_Norman_and_his_first_wife">Sir Henry Norman and his first wife</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Near_East&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Sir Henry Norman and his first wife">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The change is evident in the reports of influential British travelers to the Balkans.  In 1894, <a href="/wiki/Sir_Henry_Norman,_1st_Baronet" title="Sir Henry Norman, 1st Baronet">Sir Henry Norman, 1st Baronet</a>, a journalist, traveled to the Far East, afterwards writing a book called <i>The Peoples and Politics of the Far East</i>, which came out in 1895. By "Far East" he meant <a href="/wiki/Siberia" title="Siberia">Siberia</a>, <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Korea" title="Korea">Korea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Siam" class="mw-redirect" title="Siam">Siam</a> and <a href="/wiki/Malay_Peninsula" title="Malay Peninsula">Malaya</a>. As the book was a big success, he was off to the Balkan states with his wife in 1896 to develop detail for a sequel, <i>The People and Politics of the Near East</i>, which Scribners planned to publish in 1897. Mrs. Norman, a writer herself, wrote glowing letters of the home and person of Mme. Zakki, "the wife of a Turkish cabinet minister," who, she said, was a cultivated woman living in a country home full of books.  As for the natives of the Balkans, they were "a semi-civilized people".<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The planned book was never published, however Norman published the gist of the book, mixed with vituperation against the Ottoman Empire, in an article in June, 1896, in Scribner's Magazine.  The empire had descended from an enlightened civilization ruling over barbarians for their own good to something considerably less. The difference was the <a href="/wiki/Hamidian_Massacres" class="mw-redirect" title="Hamidian Massacres">Hamidian Massacres</a>, which were being conducted even as the couple traveled the Balkans.  According to Norman now, the empire had been established by "the Moslem horde" from <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asia</a>, which was stopped by "intrepid <a href="/wiki/Hungary" title="Hungary">Hungary</a>." Furthermore, "Greece shook off the turbaned destroyer of her people" and so on. The Russians were suddenly liberators of oppressed Balkan states. Having portrayed the Armenians as revolutionaries in the name of freedom with the expectation of being rescued by the intervention of Christian Europe, he states "but her hope was vain." England had "turned her back." Norman concluded his exhortation with "In the Balkans one learns to hate the Turk." Norman made sure that <a href="/wiki/William_Ewart_Gladstone" title="William Ewart Gladstone">Gladstone</a> read the article. Prince Nicolas of Montenegro wrote a letter thanking him for his article.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Throughout this article Norman uses "Near East" to mean the countries where "the eastern question" applied; that is, to all of the <a href="/wiki/Balkans" title="Balkans">Balkans</a>. The countries and regions mentioned are <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bulgaria" title="Bulgaria">Bulgaria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Serbia" title="Serbia">Serbia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bosnia-Herzegovina" class="mw-redirect" title="Bosnia-Herzegovina">Bosnia-Herzegovina</a> (which was <a href="/wiki/Muslim" class="mw-redirect" title="Muslim">Muslim</a> and needed, in his view, to be suppressed), <a href="/wiki/Macedonia_(region)" title="Macedonia (region)">Macedonia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Montenegro" title="Montenegro">Montenegro</a>, <a href="/wiki/Albania" title="Albania">Albania</a>, <a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania">Romania</a>. The rest of the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman</a> domain is demoted to just "the east".
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="William_Miller">William Miller</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Near_East&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: William Miller">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>If Norman was apparently attempting to change British policy, it was perhaps William Miller (1864–1945), journalist and expert on the Near East, who did the most in that direction. In essence, he signed the death warrant, so to speak, of the Age of Empires. The fall of the Ottoman Empire ultimately enmeshed all the others as well.  In the <i>Travel and Politics in the Near East</i>, 1898, Miller claimed to have made four trips to the Balkans, 1894, 1896, 1897 and 1898, and to be, in essence, an expert on "the Near East", by which he primarily meant the Balkans.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiller1898ix_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiller1898ix-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>  Apart from the fact that he attended Oxford and played <a href="/wiki/Rugby_union" title="Rugby union">Rugby</a> not many biographical details have been promulgated. He was in effect (whatever his formal associations if any) a point man of British near eastern intelligence.
</p><p>In Miller's view, the Ottoman officials were unfit to rule:<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiller1898391–392_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiller1898391–392-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The plain fact is, that it is as hard for an Ottoman official to be honest as it is for a camel to enter through the eye of a needle.  It is not so much the fault of the men as the fault of the system, which is thoroughly bad from top to bottom...  Turkish administration is synonymous with corruption, inefficiency, and sloth.
</p></blockquote>
<p>These were fighting words to be coming from a country that once insisted Europe needed Turkey and was willing to spill blood over it. For his authority Miller invokes the people, citing the "collective wisdom" of Europe, and introducing a concept to arise many times in the decades to follow under chilling circumstances: "... no final solution of the difficulty has yet been found."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiller1898479_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiller1898479-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Miller's final pronouncements on the topic could not be ignored by either the British or the Ottoman governments:<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiller1898489_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiller1898489-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>It remains then to consider whether the Great Powers can solve the Eastern Question&#160;...  Foreigners find it extremely difficult to understand the foreign, and especially the Eastern policy of Great Britain, and we cannot wonder at their difficulty, for it seems a mass of contradictions to Englishmen themselves&#160;...  At one moment we are bringing about the independence of Greece by sending the Turkish fleet to the bottom of the bay of Navarino. Twenty-seven years later we are spending immense sums and wasting thousands of lives in order to protect the Turks against Russia.
</p></blockquote>
<p>If the British Empire was now going to side with the Russian Empire, the Ottoman Empire had no choice but to cultivate a relationship with the Austro-Hungarian Empire, which was supported by the German Empire. In a few years these alignments became the <a href="/wiki/Triple_Entente" title="Triple Entente">Triple Entente</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Triple_Alliance_(1882)" title="Triple Alliance (1882)">Triple Alliance</a> (already formed in 1882), which were in part a cause of <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>. By its end in 1918 three empires were gone, a fourth was about to fall to revolution, and two more, the British and French, were forced to yield in revolutions started under the aegis of their own ideologies.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Arnold_Toynbee">Arnold Toynbee</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Near_East&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Arnold Toynbee">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-95000-347,_T%C3%BCrkei,_Anzac-Bucht,_Truppenlandung.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-95000-347%2C_T%C3%BCrkei%2C_Anzac-Bucht%2C_Truppenlandung.jpg/300px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-95000-347%2C_T%C3%BCrkei%2C_Anzac-Bucht%2C_Truppenlandung.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="224" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-95000-347%2C_T%C3%BCrkei%2C_Anzac-Bucht%2C_Truppenlandung.jpg/450px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-95000-347%2C_T%C3%BCrkei%2C_Anzac-Bucht%2C_Truppenlandung.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-95000-347%2C_T%C3%BCrkei%2C_Anzac-Bucht%2C_Truppenlandung.jpg/600px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-95000-347%2C_T%C3%BCrkei%2C_Anzac-Bucht%2C_Truppenlandung.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="596" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-95000-347,_T%C3%BCrkei,_Anzac-Bucht,_Truppenlandung.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Australian troops, Gallipoli, 1915. The battle was an Ottoman victory.</div></div></div><p>
By 1916, when millions of Europeans were becoming casualties of imperial war in the trenches of eastern and western Europe over "the eastern question", <a href="/wiki/Arnold_J._Toynbee" title="Arnold J. Toynbee">Arnold J. Toynbee</a>, Hegelesque historian of civilization at large, was becoming metaphysical about the Near East. Geography alone was not a sufficient explanation of the terms, he believed. If the Ottoman Empire had been a sick man, then:<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup></p><blockquote><p>There has been something pathological about the history of this Near Eastern World. It has had an undue share of political misfortunes, and had lain for centuries in a kind of spiritual paralysis between East and West—belonging to neither, partaking paradoxically of both, and wholly unable to rally itself decidedly to one or the other.</p></blockquote><p>
Having supposed that it was sick, he kills it off: "The Near East has never been more true to itself than in its lurid dissolution; past and present are fused together in the flare." To Toynbee the Near East was a spiritual being of a "<a href="/wiki/Janus" title="Janus">Janus</a>-character", connected to both east and west:</p><blockquote><p>The limits of the Near East are not easy to define. On the north-west, Vienna is the most conspicuous boundary-mark, but one might almost equally well single out Trieste or Lvov or even Prag. Towards the southeast, the boundaries are even more shadowy. It is perhaps best to equate them with the frontiers of the Arabic language, yet the genius of the Near East overrides linguistic barriers, and encroaches on the Arabicspeaking world on the one side as well as on the German-speaking world on the other. Syria is essentially a Near Eastern country, and a physical geographer would undoubtedly carry the Near Eastern frontiers up to the desert belt of the Sahara, Nefud and Kevir.</p></blockquote>
<p>From the death of the Near East new nations were able to rise from the ashes, notably the Republic of Turkey. Paradoxically it now aligned itself with the west rather than with the east. <a href="/wiki/Mustafa_Kemal_Atat%C3%BCrk" title="Mustafa Kemal Atatürk">Mustafa Kemal</a>, its founder, a former Ottoman high-ranking officer, was insistent on this social revolution, which, among other changes, liberated women from the strait rules still in effect in most Arabic-speaking countries. The demise of the political Near East now left a gap where it had been, into which stepped the Middle East.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rise_of_the_Middle_East">Rise of the Middle East</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Near_East&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Rise of the Middle East">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Origin_of_the_concept_of_Middle_East">Origin of the concept of Middle East</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Near_East&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Origin of the concept of Middle East">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The term <i>middle east</i> as a noun and adjective was common in the 19th century in nearly every context except diplomacy and archaeology. An uncountable number of places appear to have had their middle easts from gardens to regions, including the United States. The innovation of the term <i>Near East</i> to mean the holdings of the Ottoman Empire as early as the Crimean War had left a geographical gap. The East Indies, or "Far East", derived ultimately from <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">Ptolemy</a>'s "India Beyond the Ganges." The Ottoman Empire ended at the eastern border of Iraq. "India This Side of the Ganges" and Iran had been omitted. The archaeologists counted Iran as "the Near East" because Old Persian cuneiform had been found there. This usage did not sit well with the diplomats; India was left in an equivocal state. They needed a regional term.
</p><p>
The use of the term <i>Middle East</i> as a region of international affairs apparently began in British and American diplomatic circles quite independently of each other over concern for the security of the same country: Iran, then known to the west as Persia. In 1900 <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Edward_Gordon" title="Thomas Edward Gordon">Thomas Edward Gordon</a> published an article, <i>The Problem of the Middle East</i>, which began:<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup></p><blockquote><p>It may be assumed that the most sensitive part of our external policy in the Middle East is the preservation of the independence and integrity of Persia and Afghanistan. Our active interest in Persia began with the present century, and was due to the belief that the invasion of India by a European Power was a probable event.</p></blockquote>
<p>The threat that caused Gordon, diplomat and military officer, to publish the article was resumption of work on a railway from Russia to the Persian Gulf. Gordon, a published author, had not used the term previously, but he was to use it from then on.
</p><p>
A second strategic personality from American diplomatic and military circles, <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Thayer_Mahan" title="Alfred Thayer Mahan">Alfred Thayer Mahan</a>, concerned about the naval vulnerability of the trade routes in the <a href="/wiki/Persian_Gulf" title="Persian Gulf">Persian Gulf</a> and <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean" title="Indian Ocean">Indian Ocean</a>, commented in 1902:<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup></p><blockquote><p>The middle East, if I may adopt a term which I have not seen, will some day need its Malta, as well as its Gibraltar; it does not follow that either will be in the Gulf. Naval force has the quality of mobility which carries with it the privilege of temporary absences; but it needs to find on every scene of operation established bases of refit, of supply, and, in case of disaster, of security. The British Navy should have the facility to concentrate in force, if occasion arise, about Aden, India, and the Gulf.</p></blockquote>
<p>Apparently the sailor did not connect with the soldier, as Mahan believed he was innovating the term <i>Middle East</i>. It was, however, already there to be seen.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Single_region_concept">Single region concept</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Near_East&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Single region concept">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Until the period following World War I the Near East and the Middle East coexisted, but they were not always seen as distinct. <a href="/wiki/Bertram_Lenox_Simpson" title="Bertram Lenox Simpson">Bertram Lenox Simpson</a>, a colonial officer killed eventually in China, uses the terms together in his 1910 book, <i>The Conflict of Color</i>, as "the Near and Middle East". The total super-region consisted of "India, Afghanistan, Persia, Arabistan, Asia Minor, and last, but not least, Egypt."<sup id="cite_ref-simpson184_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-simpson184-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> Simpson (under his pen-name, Weale) explains that this entire region "is politically one region&#160;– in spite of the divisions into which it is academically divided." His own term revives <i>the Nearer East</i> as opposed to <i>the Far East</i>.
</p><p>
The basis of Simpson's unity is color and colonial subjection. His color chart recognizes a spectrum of black, brown and yellow, which at the time had been traditional since the late 19th century. Apart from these was "the great white race", which the moderate Simpson tones down to simply the white race. The great whites were appearing as late as the 1920s works of <a href="/wiki/James_Henry_Breasted" title="James Henry Breasted">James Henry Breasted</a>, which were taught as the gospel of ancient history throughout the entire first half of the 20th century. A red wavelength was mainly of interest in America. The eastern question was modified by Simpson to "The Problem of the Nearer East", which had nothing to do with the Ottomans but everything to do with British colonialism. Simpson wrote of the white man:<sup id="cite_ref-simpson184_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-simpson184-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup></p><blockquote><p>... in India, in Central Asia, and in all the regions adjacent to the Near East, he still boldly remains a conqueror in possession of vast stretches of valuable territory; a conqueror who has no intention of lightly surrendering his conquests, and who indeed sees in every attempt to modify the old order of things a most hateful and unjustifiable revolt which must at all costs be repressed. This is so absolutely true that no candid person will be inclined to dispute it.</p></blockquote>
<p>These regions were occupied by "the brown men", with the yellow in the Far East and the black in Africa. The color issue was not settled until <a href="/wiki/Kenya" title="Kenya">Kenya</a> became independent in 1963, ending the last vestige of the British Empire.
</p><p>This view reveals a somewhat less than altruistic Christian intent of the British Empire; however, it was paradoxical from the beginning, as Simpson and most other writers pointed out. The Ottomans were portrayed as the slavers, but even as the American and British fleets were striking at the Barbary pirates on behalf of freedom, their countries were promulgating a vigorous African slave trade of their own. <a href="/wiki/Charles_George_Gordon" title="Charles George Gordon">Charles George Gordon</a> is known as the saint of all British colonial officers. A dedicated Christian, he spent his time between assignments living among the poor and donating his salary on their behalf. He won Ottoman confidence as a junior officer in the Crimean War. In his later career he became a high official in the Ottoman Empire, working as Governor of Egypt for the Ottoman <a href="/wiki/Khedive" title="Khedive">khedive</a> for the purpose of conducting campaigns against slavers and slavery in Egypt and the Sudan.
</p>
<h4><span id="One_presumed_region.2C_one_name"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="One_presumed_region,_one_name">One presumed region, one name</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Near_East&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: One presumed region, one name">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The term <i>Near and Middle East</i>, held the stage for a few years before World War I. It proved to be less acceptable to a colonial point of view that saw the entire region as one. In 1916 Captain T.&#160;C. Fowle, 40th Pathans (troops of British India), wrote of a trip he had taken from <a href="/wiki/Karachi" title="Karachi">Karachi</a> to <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a> just before the war. The book does not contain a single instance of "Near East". Instead, the entire region is considered "the Middle East".<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> The formerly Near Eastern sections of his trip are now "Turkish" and not Ottoman.
</p><p>Subsequently, with the disgrace of <i>Near East</i> in diplomatic and military circles, <i>Middle East</i> prevailed. However, <i>Near East</i> continues in some circles at the discretion of the defining agency or academic department. They are not generally considered distinct regions as they were at their original definition.
</p><p>Although racial and colonial definitions of the Middle East are no longer considered ideologically sound, the sentiment of unity persists. For much, but by no means all, of the Middle East, the predominance of Islam lends some unity, as does the transient accident of geographical continuity. Otherwise there is but little basis except for history and convention to lump together peoples of multiple, often unrelated languages, governments, loyalties and customs.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Current_meaning">Current meaning</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Near_East&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Current meaning">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Diplomatic">Diplomatic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Near_East&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Diplomatic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the 20th century, subsequent to major warfare and decades of intense political turmoil, the terms, such as <i>Near East</i>, <i>Far East</i>, and <i>Middle East</i> continued to be used, but evolved in their meaning and scope. This increased confusion, the resolution of which became the study of experts in the new field of <a href="/wiki/Political_science" title="Political science">political science</a>. The new wave of diplomats often came from those programmes.
</p><p>Archaeology on the international scene, though very much of intellectual interest to major universities, was overshadowed by international relations. The archaeologists' domain became the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Near_East" title="Ancient Near East">Ancient Near East</a>, which could no longer be relied upon to be the actual Near East. The Ottoman Empire was gone, along with all the other empires of the 19th century, replaced in the region with a number of republics with various affinities, regional and global.
</p><p>The many and varied specialized agencies that were formed to handle specific aspects of complex international relations, evolved with the terms. Definitions from the present came to be not in concert with those of the past. Reconciling these terms and their definitions remains difficult due to ongoing territorial disputes and non-free nuclear powers' territorial ambitions, putting any reconciliation of definitions out of scope of diplomatic corps in the classical sense.
</p><p>The ancient Near East is frozen in time. The living Near East is primarily what the agencies each define as a matter of practice; often guided by their political leadership. In most cases, this single term is inadequate to describe the geographical range in practical applications. This has resulted is multiple definitions used differently by each major region, power, or instituation.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Influential_agencies_represented_in_the_table">Influential agencies represented in the table</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Near_East&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Influential agencies represented in the table">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:142px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bureau_of_Near_Eastern_Affairs.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/51/Bureau_of_Near_Eastern_Affairs.png" decoding="async" width="140" height="160" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="140" data-file-height="160" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bureau_of_Near_Eastern_Affairs.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Logotype of the Bureau of Near Eastern Affairs</div></div></div>
<p>The United States is the chief remaining nation to assign official responsibilities to a region called the Near East. Within the government the State Department has been most influential in promulgating the Near Eastern regional system. The countries of the former empires of the 19th century have in general abandoned the term and the subdivision in favor of Middle East, North Africa, and various forms of Asia. In many cases, such as France, no distinct regional substructures have been employed. Each country has its own French diplomatic apparatus, although regional terms, including Proche-Orient and Moyen-Orient, can be used in a descriptive sense.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>Some of the most influential agencies in the United States still use Near East as a working concept. For example, the <a href="/wiki/Bureau_of_Near_Eastern_Affairs" title="Bureau of Near Eastern Affairs">Bureau of Near Eastern Affairs</a>, a division of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_State" title="United States Department of State">United States Department of State</a>, is perhaps the most influential agency to still use the term <i>Near East</i>. Under the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_State" title="United States Secretary of State">Secretary of State</a>, it implements the official <a href="/wiki/Diplomacy" title="Diplomacy">diplomacy</a> of the United States, called also statecraft by Secretary <a href="/wiki/Hillary_Clinton" title="Hillary Clinton">Hillary Clinton</a>. The name of the bureau is traditional and historic. There is, however, no distinct Middle East. All official Middle Eastern affairs are referred to this bureau.<sup id="cite_ref-state_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-state-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Working closely in conjunction with the definition of the Near East provided by the State Department is the <a href="/wiki/Near_East_South_Asia_Center_for_Strategic_Studies" title="Near East South Asia Center for Strategic Studies">Near East South Asia Center for Strategic Studies</a> (NESA), an educational institution of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Defense" title="United States Department of Defense">United States Department of Defense</a>. It teaches courses and holds seminars and workshops for government officials and military officers who will work or are working within its region. As the name indicates, that region is a combination of State Department regions; however, NESA is careful to identify the State Department region.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> As its Near East is not different from the State Department's it does not appear in the table. Its name, however, is not entirely accurate. For example, its region includes Mauritania, a member of the State Department's Africa (Sub-Sahara).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Washington_Institute_for_Near_East_Policy" class="mw-redirect" title="Washington Institute for Near East Policy">Washington Institute for Near East Policy</a> (WINEP) is a non-profit organization for research and advice on Middle Eastern policy. It regards its target countries as the Middle East but adopts the convention of calling them the Near East to be in conformance with the practices of the State Department. Its views are independent.<sup id="cite_ref-institute_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-institute-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> The WINEP bundles the countries of Northwest Africa together under "North Africa". Details can be found in <i>Policy Focus #65</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_regional_systems">Other regional systems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Near_East&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Other regional systems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> formulates multiple regional divisions as is convenient for its various operations. But few of them include a Near East, and that poorly defined. <a href="/wiki/UNICEF" title="UNICEF">UNICEF</a> recognizes the "Middle East and North Africa" region, where the Middle East is bounded by the <a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">Red Sea</a> on the west and includes Iran on the east.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">UNESCO</a> recognizes neither a Near East nor a Middle East, dividing the countries instead among three regions: Arab States, Asia and the Pacific, and Africa. Its division "does not forcibly reflect geography" but "refers to the execution of regional activities."<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Statistics_Division" title="United Nations Statistics Division">United Nations Statistics Division</a> defines <a href="/wiki/Western_Asia" title="Western Asia">Western Asia</a> to contain the countries included elsewhere in the Middle East.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> Its total area extends further into Central Asia than that of most agencies.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Central_Intelligence_Agency" title="Central Intelligence Agency">Central Intelligence Agency</a> (CIA) is a quasi-independent agency of the United States Government. It appears to have multiple leadership. On the one hand its director is appointed by the president. It plays a significant role in providing the president with intelligence. On the other hand, Congress oversees its operations through a committee. The CIA was first formed under the <a href="/wiki/National_Security_Act_of_1947" title="National Security Act of 1947">National Security Act of 1947</a> from the army's <a href="/wiki/Office_of_Strategic_Services" title="Office of Strategic Services">Office of Strategic Services</a> (OSS), which furnished both military intelligence and clandestine military operations to the army during the crisis of <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>. Many revisions and redefinitions have taken place since then. Although the name of the CIA reflects the original advised intent of Presidents <a href="/wiki/Franklin_D._Roosevelt" title="Franklin D. Roosevelt">Franklin D. Roosevelt</a> and <a href="/wiki/Harry_S._Truman" title="Harry S. Truman">Harry S. Truman</a>, the government's needs for strategic services have frustrated that intent from the beginning. The press received by the agency in countless articles, novels and other media have tended to create various popular myths; for example, that this agency replaced any intelligence effort other than that of the OSS, or that it contains the central intelligence capability of the United States. Strategic services are officially provided by some 17 agencies called the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Intelligence_Community" title="United States Intelligence Community">Intelligence Community</a>. Army intelligence did not come to an end; in fact, all the branches of the Armed Forces retained their intelligence services. This community is currently under the leadership (in addition to all its other leadership) of the <a href="/wiki/Office_of_the_Director_of_National_Intelligence" class="mw-redirect" title="Office of the Director of National Intelligence">Office of the Director of National Intelligence</a>.
</p><p>Under these complex circumstances regional names are less useful. They are more historical than an accurate gauge of operations. The Directorate of Intelligence, one of four directorates into which the CIA is divided, includes the Office of Near Eastern and South Asian Analysis (NESA). Its duties are defined as "support on Middle Eastern and North African countries, as well as on the South Asian nations of India, Pakistan, and Afghanistan."<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> The total range of countries is in fact the same as the State Department's Near East, but the names do not correspond. The Near East of the NESA is the same as the Middle East defined in the CIA-published on-line resource, <i>The World Factbook</i>. Its list of countries is limited by the Red Sea, comprises the entire eastern coast of the Mediterranean, including Israel, Turkey, the small nations of the Caucasus, Iran and the states of the Arabian Peninsula.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Agency_for_International_Development" title="United States Agency for International Development">U.S. Agency for International Development</a> (USAID), an independent agency under the Department of State established in place of the <a href="/wiki/Marshall_Plan" title="Marshall Plan">Marshall Plan</a> for the purpose of determining and distributing <a href="/wiki/Foreign_aid" class="mw-redirect" title="Foreign aid">foreign aid</a>, does not use the term <i>Near East</i>. Its definition of Middle East corresponds to that of the State Department, which officially prefers the term <i>Near East</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-USAID_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USAID-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Foreign_and_Commonwealth_Office" title="Foreign and Commonwealth Office">Foreign and Commonwealth Office</a> of United Kingdom recognises a Middle East and North Africa region, but not a Near East. Their original Middle East consumed the Near East as far as the Red Sea, ceded India to the Asia and Oceania region, and went into partnership with North Africa as far as the Atlantic.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Ministry of Foreign Affairs of the Republic of <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a> conducts "bilateral relationships" with the countries of the "Mediterranean – Middle East Region" but has formulated no Near East Region.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> The Ministry of Foreign Affairs of the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Turkey" class="mw-redirect" title="Republic of Turkey">Republic of Turkey</a> also does not use the term <i>Near East</i>. Its regions include the Middle East, the Balkans and others.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Archaeological">Archaeological</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Near_East&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Archaeological">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Near_East" title="Ancient Near East">Ancient Near East</a> is a term of the 20th century intended to stabilize the geographical application of Near East to ancient history.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> The Near East may acquire varying meanings, but the Ancient Near East always has the same meaning: the ancient nations, people and languages of the enhanced <a href="/wiki/Fertile_Crescent" title="Fertile Crescent">Fertile Crescent</a>, a sweep of land from the Nile Valley through Anatolia and southward to the limits of <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a>.
</p><p>Resorting to this verbal device, however, did not protect the "Ancient Near East" from the inroads of "the Middle East". For example, a high point in the use of "Ancient Near East" was for Biblical scholars the <i>Ancient Near Eastern Texts relating to the Old Testament</i> by <a href="/wiki/James_B._Pritchard" title="James B. Pritchard">James Bennett Pritchard</a>, a textbook of first edition dated 1950. The last great book written by <a href="/wiki/Leonard_Woolley" title="Leonard Woolley">Leonard Woolley</a>, British archaeologist, excavator of ancient Ur and associate of <a href="/wiki/T._E._Lawrence" title="T. E. Lawrence">T. E. Lawrence</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Evans" title="Arthur Evans">Arthur Evans</a>, was <i>The Art of the Middle East, Including Persia, Mesopotamia and Palestine</i>, published in 1961. Woolley had completed it in 1960 two weeks before his death. The geographical ranges in each case are identical.
</p><p>Parallel with the growth of specialized agencies for conducting or supporting statescraft in the second half of the 20th century has been the collection of resources for scholarship and research typically in university settings. Most universities teaching the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_arts" class="mw-redirect" title="Liberal arts">liberal arts</a> have library and museum collections. These are not new; however, the erection of these into "centres" of national and international interest in the second half of the 20th century have created larger databases not available to the scholars of the past. Many of these focus on the Ancient Near East or Near East in the sense of Ancient Near East.
</p><p>One such institution is the Centre for the Study of Ancient Documents (CSAD) founded by and located centrally at <a href="/wiki/Oxford_University" class="mw-redirect" title="Oxford University">Oxford University</a>, Great Britain. Among its many activities CSAD numbers "a long-term project to create a library of digitised images of Greek inscriptions." These it arranges by region. The Egypt and the Near East region besides Egypt includes Cyprus, Persia and Afghanistan but not Asia Minor (a separate region).<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Academic">Academic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Near_East&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Academic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A large percentage of experts on the modern <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a> began their training in university departments named for the Near East. Similarly the journals associated with these fields of expertise include the words Near East or Near Eastern. The meaning of Near East in these numerous establishments and publications is Middle East. Expertise on the modern Middle East is almost never mixed or confused with studies of the Ancient Near East, although often "Ancient Near East" is abbreviated to "Near East" without any implication of modern times. For example, "Near Eastern Languages" in the ancient sense includes such languages as <a href="/wiki/Sumerian_language" title="Sumerian language">Sumerian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Akkadian_(language)" class="mw-redirect" title="Akkadian (language)">Akkadian</a>. In the modern sense, it is likely to mean any or all of the <a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic languages</a>.
</p>
